Testing Claims with Implicative Reasoning
Buti demonstrates how to use implicative reasoning to verify or falsify claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how implicative reasoning can be used to evaluate the truth or falsehood of a claim by examining its entailments.

Core Idea
Check a claim by looking for what it implies; if the implications are absent or opposite, the claim is false.

Buti extends the application of implicative reasoning to everyday claims. When someone makes a statement, we can test its truth by searching for its logical entailments (mustalzimat). If those entailments are observed, the claim is likely true; if they are missing or contradictory, the claim is false. He provides two concrete examples: a village claim and a theory about human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the method for testing claims
Read the part where Buti says 'وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى' (that is by searching for the entailments of that claim). Understand that we look for what the claim logically implies.
Entailments are the necessary consequences or signs of the claim being true.
2
Analyze the village example
Read the example: a man claims all villagers are Muslims, but we see crosses on houses. The crosses (entailment of non-Muslim faith) contradict the claim. So the claim is false.
The crosses are a signifier of Christianity, not Islam.
3
Analyze the thought origin example
Read the example: someone claims thought originates from need for food. But animals share the need for food but lack thought. Thus, the entailment (that need for food leads to thought) is absent in animals, disproving the claim.
This is a counterexample using the same method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالدال في مثل هذه الأمثلة كلها ليس علة للمدلول، حتى نقول إن دلالته عليه من قبيل دلالة العلة على المعلول؛ إذ النحول ليس علة للمرض، والمآذن الصاعدة ليست علة لإسلام أهل البلدة، وصوت الصفارة ليس علة لحدوث الحريق، وعربدة الرجل ليست هي العلة لشربه المسكر. وأنت عندما تشاهد الدالّ في هذه الأمثلة لا تبصر معها المدلول ولا تشاهده حتى تقول إن الدليل هو الرؤية والمشاهدة، بل هو شيء خفي عن مشاهدتك وإحساسك. إذاً فكيف دلت هذه الأشياء على مدلولاتها، وكيف نؤمن بها دون أن نراها؟!، إن سبيل الدلالة أنها عُرفت بملازمتها الدائمة لملزوماتها. وتكرر ذلك دون تخلف، وتم على ذلك الاستقراء، فتكونت من هذه المقارنة الدائمة رابطة دلالة سارية بينهما. وسبيل الاستفادة من هذا البرهان يكون، بأن تتأمل في ظاهرة ما، تشاهدها أمامك، فإن رأيت - عن طريق دلالة الاستقراء - أن تلك الظاهرة تستلزم حقيقة معينة، كان من الطبيعي في ميزان العقل أن تؤمن بها، ولو لم تجدها ماثلة أمام عينيك؛ فإن الذي يرى سيارة الإسعاف تنهب الأرض وهي بصفيرها المتواصل، لا يشك أن ثمة مريضاً يعاني من حالة خطيرة على حياته وإن لم يكن يراه بعينيه، بل لعله لا ينتبه الحقيقة السيارة التي تمر أمام عينيه بمقدار ما ينتبه لحالة المريض التي تقفز إذ ذاك إلى ذهنه. وإذا طرح أحدهم أمامك دعوى يزعمها، فإنك كثيراً ما تستطيع بواسطة دلالة الالتزام هذه أن تعلم صدقها أو بطلانها. وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى، فإن رأيت هذه المستلزمات ماثلة أمامك كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق الدعوى، وإن كانت مفقودة أو كان الموجود نقيضها، كان ذلك دليلاً على كذب الدعوى فالرجل الذي يشرف بك على قرية ويقول لك: إن جميع أهلها مسلمون، لا يمكن أن تصدق كلامه هذا، إذا تأملت فلم تجد فوق بيوتها إلا صلبان الكنائس، على الرغم من أنك لم تجد أحداً من أهلها ولم تقف على اعتقاداتهم وما يدينون به عن طريق التجربة والمشاهدة. والذي يدعي لك أن منبع نشأة الفكر والعقل في الإنسان إنما هو شعوره بالحاجة إلى الغذاء، لا يمكنك أن تصدق دعواه، إذا تأملت في سائر الحيوانات التي تشترك مع الإنسان في الشعور بالحاجة إلى الغذاء دون أن يتكون لديها هي الأخرى شيء من التفكير والعقل.
The signifier in all such examples is not a cause of the signified, such that we say its signification of it is of the type of signification of a cause for an effect; for emaciation is not a cause of illness, and the rising minarets are not a cause of the Islam of the people of the town, and the sound of the siren is not a cause of the occurrence of the fire, and a man's drunken behavior is not the cause of his drinking intoxicants. And when you witness the signifier in these examples, you do not perceive the signified with it nor do you observe it, such that you would say the evidence is sight and observation; rather, it is something hidden from your observation and sensation. So how then did these things indicate their significates, and how do we believe in them without seeing them?! The way of signification is that it was known by their constant accompaniment of their concomitants. And that is repeated without exception, and induction has been carried out accordingly, so from this constant comparison a relation of signification arises between them. And the way of benefiting from this proof is that you reflect on a phenomenon that you witness before you, and if you see—by way of inductive signification—that that phenomenon entails a certain reality, it is natural in the balance of the intellect to believe in it, even if you do not find it manifest before your eyes; for he who sees an ambulance racing on the ground with its continuous siren does not doubt that there is a patient suffering from a critical condition threatening his life, even if he does not see him with his own eyes—indeed, perhaps he does not pay attention to the actual vehicle passing before his eyes as much as he pays attention to the patient's condition that jumps into his mind at that moment. And if someone puts forward a claim he asserts, you can often, by means of this implicative signification, know its truth or falsehood. That is by searching for the entailments of that claim: if you see these entailments manifest before you, that is evidence of the truth of the claim; and if they are absent or their opposite is present, that is evidence of the falsehood of the claim. The man who looks down upon a village and says to you: 'All its inhabitants are Muslims,' you cannot believe his statement if you reflect and find nothing above its houses but crosses of churches, despite the fact that you have not met any of its inhabitants nor learned of their beliefs and what they adhere to through experiment and observation. And the one who claims to you that the source of the origin of thought and intellect in man is only his feeling of need for food, you cannot believe his claim if you reflect on the rest of the animals that share with man the feeling of need for food without there developing in them as well any thought or intellect.

Classification of Implicative Signification
Buti explains the threefold classification of dalala al-luzum with two types detailed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of implicative signification (dalala al-luzum) and the distinguishing features of the first two types: non-evident and evident in the general sense.

Core Idea
Implicative signification varies in evidentiary strength; some need proof, others just reflection.

In this passage, Buti explains that not all implicative relations yield certainty. The strength of an implication depends on how clearly the concomitant relationship is perceived. Scholars therefore divided implicative signification into three levels, from weakest to strongest. Here, we explore the first two types, which illustrate how some implications require external proof while others become clear upon careful reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's structure: an introductory statement, then a threefold division, then detailed description of the first two types.
The passage cuts off at the beginning of the third type; focus on the two complete types.
2
Identify the classification principle
Find the criterion used to divide implicative signification. The author says the division is based on 'the degree of clarity and independence from another proof'.
This criterion creates a hierarchy of evidentiary strength.
3
Understand the first type: non-evident implication
Study the definition and the triangle example. Why does this type require another proof? Why is it only part of a proof?
The triangle example shows that without geometric proof, the implication is not certain.
4
Understand the second type: evident implication (general sense)
Read the definition and the beginning example. Notice that it only requires conception and deep reflection, not an external proof.
The author mentions a contingent thing as an example, but the passage ends before full explanation.
5
Compare the two types
Contrast the two: one needs an external proof, the other is self-sufficient after reflection. Consider why this distinction matters for theological arguments.
This helps assess the strength of implicative arguments in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع دلالة الالتزام: إلا أن هذا التلازم لا يورث اليقين دائماً، إذ الأمر فيه منوط بمدى وضوح التلازم واستغنائه عن برهان آخر يدل عليه، ولذلك قسم العلماء دلالة اللزوم إلى ثلاثة أقسام، ترتقي في القوة من الأدنى إلى الأعلى: أولاها ما يسمى باللزوم غير البيّن: وهو أن يتوقف الجزم بوجود اللزوم فيه على إقامة برهان آخر، كالتزام زوايا المثلث لقائمتين، فإن العقل لا يجزم بذلك لكل مثلث مالم يطلع على برهان آخر مثبت له كتصور الدائرة ومعرفة درجاتها. ومن ثم فإن هذا التلازم وحده لا يعتبر دليلاً لأنه هو نفسه يحتاج إلى برهان ودليل عليه، ولكنه يعتبر جزءاً من دليل، يتكامل بضم تتمته إليه. ثانيها مايسمى باللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم: وهو أن يتوقف إدراك اللزوم بين الشيئين على تصور كل منها والنظر فيه ملياً، كدلالة الشيء الممكن
Types of implicative signification: However, this concomitance does not always yield certainty, for the matter depends on the degree of clarity of the concomitance and its independence from another proof indicating it. Therefore, the scholars divided the signification of implication (dalala al-luzum) into three divisions, ascending in strength from the lowest to the highest: First of them is what is called non-evident implication (al-luzum ghayr al-bayyin): it is that judgement of the existence of the implication is dependent on establishing another proof, such as the implication of the angles of a triangle for two right angles; for the intellect does not judge that for every triangle unless it is informed of another proof affirming it, such as the conception of the circle and knowledge of its degrees. Therefore, this concomitance by itself is not considered a proof because it itself needs a proof and evidence for it, but it is considered a part of a proof, completed by adding its complement to it. Second of them is what is called evident implication in the general sense (al-luzum al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm): it is that perception of the implication between two things is dependent on conceiving each of them and deeply reflecting on them, such as the signification of a contingent thing...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of implicative signification (dalala al-luzum). The author states that concomitance does not always produce certainty; it depends on how clear the concomitance is and whether it needs another proof. Therefore, scholars divided it into three types, ordered by strength. The first type is 'non-evident implication' (al-luzum ghayr al-bayyin): here, judgment of the implication requires another proof. The example is the triangle's angles summing to two right angles, which is not self-evident to the intellect without a geometric proof. This type alone is not a proof but a part of a proof. The second type is 'evident implication in the general sense' (al-luzum al-bayyin bi'l-ma'na al-a'mm): here, perceiving the implication requires conceiving both things and reflecting deeply on them. The author begins an example (a contingent thing) but the passage is cut off. The classification helps the student understand that different implicative arguments have different evidentiary weight, and some need additional support.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All implicative significations are equally certain.
Correction: Implicative signification varies in evidentiary strength: some types require external proof and are less certain, while others are self-evident after reflection.

Checkpoint

A) Non-evident implication is always false, while evident is always true.
B) Non-evident implication requires another proof to establish it, while evident only requires conceiving and reflecting on the two things.
C) Non-evident implication is based on sense perception, while evident is based on intuition.
D) Non-evident implication is specific to geometric truths, while evident applies only to physical objects.

Key Takeaway
The strength of an implicative argument depends on whether the implication is self-evident after reflection or requires an external proof; this classification helps assess its reliability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Reflect on your own thought process: Have you ever assumed that something is necessarily true just because it 'feels' connected, without verifying the logical link? How might this classification help you become more precise in your understanding of Islamic beliefs and shield you from shaky arguments?

Implicative Signification and Its Verification Method
Defines implicative signification and its verification method with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) works as a method for verifying claims by examining their necessary implications.

Core Idea
Implicative signification judges a claim by its necessary implications.

In this passage, the author introduces a powerful method for evaluating truth claims without direct observation: implicative signification. Instead of proving a claim directly, you examine its unavoidable consequences. If those consequences are present, the claim is likely true; if absent or opposite, the claim is false. The passage illustrates this with clear examples from village inhabitants and the origin of human thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the concise style and the use of concrete examples.
2
Identify the method
Locate where the author states the general method of implicative signification. Understand the two conditions: presence of implications indicates truth; absence or opposite indicates falsehood.
The method is stated in the first two sentences.
3
Examine the first example
Consider the village example. How do the crosses on houses serve as an implication that contradicts the claim? This shows how observable implications can disprove a claim without direct verification.
4
Examine the second example
Consider the claim about thought arising from hunger. How does the existence of animals that feel hunger but lack thought refute the claim? This shows how implications from a broader set of facts can disprove a generalization.
5
Reflect on the concept
Think about how this method applies in everyday reasoning or theological arguments. Why is it important to identify necessary implications of a claim?
This helps avoid relying on direct evidence when it is unavailable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دلالة الالتزام هذه أن تعلم صدقها أو بطلانها . وذلك عن طريق البحث عن مستلزمات تلك الدعوى ، فإن رأيت هذه المستلزمات ماثلة أمامك كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق الدعوى ، وإن كانت مفقودة أو كان الموجود نقيضها ، كان ذلك دليلاً على كذب الدعوى فالرجل الذي يشرف بك على قرية ويقول لك : إن جميع أهلها مسلمون ، لا يمكن أن تصدق كلامه هذا ، إذا تأملت فلم تجد فـوق بـيـوتـهـا إلا صلبان الكنائس ، على الرغم من أنك لم تجد أحداً من أهلها ولم تقف على اعتقاداتهم وما يدينون به عن طريق التجربة والمشاهدة. والذي يدعي لك أن منبع نشأة الفكر والعقل في الإنسان إنما هو شعوره بالحاجة إلى الغذاء ، لا يمكنك أن تصدق دعواه ، إذا تأملت في سائر الحيوانات التي تشترك مع الإنسان في الشعور بالحاجة إلى الغذاء دون أن يتكون لديها هي الأخرى شيء من التفكير والعقل .
This implicative signification is that you know its truth or falsehood. And that is by investigating the implications of that claim. If you see these implications present before you, that is evidence of the truth of the claim. If they are absent or their opposite is present, that is evidence of the falsehood of the claim. So the man who makes you overlook a village and says to you: All its inhabitants are Muslims, you cannot believe his statement, if you ponder and find nothing above its houses but crosses of churches, despite not having seen any of its inhabitants and not having learned their beliefs and what they adhere to through experience and observation. And the one who claims to you that the source of the emergence of thought and intellect in humans is only their feeling of need for food, you cannot believe his claim, if you ponder on the other animals that share with humans the feeling of need for food without developing any of thought or intellect.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage defines implicative signification (dalalat al-iltizam) as the means to know the truth or falsehood of a claim by investigating its necessary implications (mustalzimat). It first states the general method: if the implications are present, the claim is true; if absent or opposite, false. Then it provides two examples. The first: a man claims all villagers are Muslims, but upon inspection you see only crosses on houses, implying they are not Muslims, even without direct observation. The second: a claim that thought arises only from the need for food is refuted by noting that animals share that need but lack thought. Both examples illustrate how implications can disprove claims without empirical verification of the claim itself.

Classification of Implicative Signification and the First Type: Non-Evident Implication
Classifies implicative signification into three types and describes the first: non-evident implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars classify implicative signification into three types and why the first type, non-evident implication, requires an external proof to establish certainty.

Core Idea
Implicative signification varies in strength; the weakest type needs another proof.

Not every implication automatically yields certainty. Scholars have categorized implicative signification based on the clarity and independence of the logical connection. The lowest category, non-evident implication, serves as a component of proof rather than a complete proof itself. This lesson introduces this classification and unpacks the first type.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the general principle
The passage begins by noting that implication does not always yield certainty; it depends on clarity. Understand this foundational idea.
2
Identify the three-type classification
Scholars divided implicative signification into three ascending tiers. Focus on the first: non-evident implication.
3
Examine the example of triangle angles
The example illustrates that the necessary connection (triangle angles summing to 180 degrees) is not obvious; it requires a geometric proof.
The example uses the circle method to prove the sum of angles.
4
Apply the conclusion
Recognize that non-evident implication alone is not a proof but a part of one, needing completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنواع دلالة الالتزام : إلا أن هذا التلازم لا يورث اليقين دائماً ، إذ الأمر فيه منوط بمدى وضوح التلازم واستغنائه عن برهان آخر يدل عليه ، ولذلك قسم العلماء دلالة اللزوم إلى ثلاثة أقسام ، ترتقي في القوة من الأدنى إلى الأعلى : أولاها ما يسمى باللزوم غير البيّن : وهو أن يتوقف الجزم بوجود اللزوم فيه على إقامة برهان آخر ، كالتزام زوايا المثلث لقائمتين ، فإن العقل لا يجزم بذلك لكل مثلث مالم يطلع على برهان آخر مثبت لـه كتصور الدائرة ومعرفة درجاتها . ومن ثم فإن هذا التلازم وحده لا يعتبر دليلاً لأنه هو نفسه يحتاج إلى برهان ودليل عليه ، ولكنه يعتبر جزءاً من دليل ، يتكامل بضم تتمته إليه
Types of implicative signification: However, this implication does not always produce certainty, because the matter depends on the clarity of the implication and its independence from another proof indicating it. Therefore, the scholars divided implicative signification into three types, ascending in strength from lowest to highest: First: what is called non-evident implication: that certainty of the existence of the implication depends on presenting another proof, like the implication of the angles of a triangle being two right angles, for the intellect does not judge that for every triangle unless it learns of another proof proving it, such as conceiving the circle and knowing its degrees. Hence, this implication alone is not considered a proof because it itself needs a proof and evidence for it, but it is considered a part of a proof, completed by joining its complement to it.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage begins by noting that not all implications produce certainty; it depends on clarity and independence from another proof. Scholars classified implication into three types ascending in strength. The first type is luzum ghayr bayyin (non-evident implication). For this type, certainty of the necessary connection requires an additional proof. The example is the implication that the angles of a triangle equal two right angles; the intellect cannot judge this for every triangle without a geometric proof (e.g., using a circle and its degrees). Therefore, this implication alone is not a complete proof but a component that needs complementation.

Key Terms
تلازم
لزوم غير بيّن
برهان
زوايا المثلث
قائمتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-evident implication is useless and provides no value.
Correction: The passage clarifies that non-evident implication is a component of a proof; it is not useless but requires external evidence to become complete.

Checkpoint
What characterizes the 'لزوم غير بيّن' (non-evident implication)?
A) It produces certainty immediately
B) It requires another proof to establish the necessity
C) It is the strongest type
D) It is based on direct observation

Key Takeaway
Non-evident implication is a necessary but incomplete part of a proof; it must be supplemented by an external evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith do you rely on an implication that you have never verified with a separate proof? What would that proof look like, and are you willing to seek it?
?
The passage teaches that some implications need external proof to yield certainty. Is there an article of your belief that you hold based on implication alone? How can you strengthen it with evidence from revelation or reason?
?
Consider the triangle example: the sum of angles is not self-evident but requires geometric demonstration. Are there truths about Allah's attributes or actions that you accept without rational or textual demonstration? How does this classification challenge you to go deeper?

Evident Implication in the Broader Sense
Defines and exemplifies the second type of implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of logical implication—evident in the broader sense—where deep reflection on the meanings alone suffices to perceive the connection, without needing an extra proof.

Core Idea
Deep reflection on meanings reveals the connection; no extra proof needed.

After introducing the most direct implication, Imam al-Būṭī now presents the second type, which requires careful thought but not a separate proof. This type, 'evident in the broader sense,' shows how understanding key concepts like possibility and necessity naturally leads to recognizing relationships such as creation and eternity. Let us examine the definition and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has a definition followed by two examples.
2
Identify the definition
Find the sentence that defines the second type. What is the condition for perceiving the implication?
The condition is that you need to conceive each thing and examine it thoroughly.
3
Examine the first example
Consider the example of a possible thing (mumkin) implying it is created (hadith). Why does this require deep reflection?
You must understand what possibility means: that existence is not necessary and depends on a cause.
4
Examine the second example
Now consider the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) implying eternity (qadim). How is this similar to the first example?
Both require you to grasp the meanings fully before the implication becomes clear.
5
Contrast with non-evident implication
Notice the author's closing remark: 'you do not need to conceive another proof'—this contrasts with the third type (non-evident) which will be introduced next.
This helps you understand the classification of implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانيها مايسمى باللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأعم : وهو أن يتوقف إدراك اللزوم بين الشيئين على تصور كل منها والنظر فيه ملياً ، كدلالة الشيء الممكن على أنه حادث ، وكدلالة واجب الوجود على أنه قديم ، فإنك لاتفهم لزوم الممكنات لصفة الحدوث إلا إذا أمعنت النظر في معنى الإمكان ، وأدركت أنــه الشيء الذي لايحيل العقل فقدانه وإنما ترجح فيه جانب الوجود المرجح طارئ ، ثم أمعنت النظر في معنى الحدوث وتصورت الصلة بينه وبين كل الممكنات التي من شأنها أن توجد بتأثير غيرها ولكنك على كل حال لاتحتاج إلى تصور برهان آخر ( كما هو شأن اللزوم غير البين ) لثبوت هذا اللزوم
Second: what is called evident implication in the broader sense: that perceiving the implication between two things depends on conceiving each of them and examining them thoroughly, like the implication of a possible thing indicating that it is created, and the implication of the necessary existent indicating that it is eternal. For you do not understand the implication of possibles for the attribute of creation except if you delve into the meaning of possibility and realize that it is something whose absence the intellect does not deem impossible, but the aspect of existence is determined by a supervening determinant; then you delve into the meaning of creation and conceive the connection between it and all possibles that tend to exist through the influence of another. But in any case, you do not need to conceive another proof (as is the case with non-evident implication) to establish this implication.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of implication: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-a'amm (evident in the broader sense). To perceive this implication, one must conceive and thoroughly examine each of the two terms, but no additional proof is needed. The first example: a possible thing (mumkin) implies it is created (hadith), because possibility means its existence is not necessary but dependent on a cause. The second example: the Necessary Existent (Wajib al-Wujud) implies eternity (qadim). Understanding the meanings of 'possibility' and 'creation' reveals the connection. Unlike the first type, this does not require an external proof, only careful reflection.

Key Terms
لزوم بيّن بالمعنى الأعم
ممكن
حادث
واجب الوجود
قديم
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this type requires no thought; actually it requires deep reflection.
Correction: The passage says 'perceiving the implication depends on conceiving each of them and examining them thoroughly'—it is not immediate like the first type; you must reflect deeply on the meanings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, to perceive the implication between a possible thing and its createdness, one needs to:
A) Consult a separate proof
B) Conceive and deeply reflect on the meanings of possibility and creation
C) Observe empirical evidence
D) Rely on authority

Key Takeaway
Evident implication in the broader sense becomes clear through deep reflection on the meanings of the terms, without need for additional proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own existence, do you casually accept it or have you truly reflected on what it means for you to be a possible being? What would change in your heart if you spent an hour tonight contemplating that you exist only because of a Creator?
?
The lesson shows that the link between a possible being and its createdness becomes evident after deep examination. Have you ever truly examined the meaning of 'creation' as applied to yourself? What is stopping you from doing that right now?
?
The Necessary Existent implies eternity. Do your daily actions reflect that Allah is the Eternal, or do you live as if He is temporary in your concerns? How would your priorities shift if you internalized that He alone is necessary and everlasting?

The Third Type: Evident Implication in the Narrower Sense
Defines the strongest implication type with examples and ranks it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what makes an implication 'evident in the narrower sense' and why it is the strongest type of implication in logical reasoning.

Core Idea
The strongest proof is when merely conceiving the sign suffices for certainty about what it implies.

We have seen three types of implication. Now we examine the strongest: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass. In this type, the connection between sign and meaning is so clear that no further thought is needed. The mind immediately jumps to the conclusion. This passage defines it clearly with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the key term 'luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass' and how the author describes it as requiring only the implicant to be conceived.
2
Analyze the examples
Consider each example: ambulance->patient, groaning->illness, voice in darkness->living being. Notice how in each case the connection is immediate and certain.
These examples are from natural and intellectual matters.
3
Compare with other types
The author ends by ranking the three types. Identify why this third type is strongest, and why the first type alone is not a proof.
This reinforces the hierarchy of implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثها اللزوم البيّن بالمعنى الأخص : وهو أن يكون تصور الملزوم وحده كافياً في تصور اللزوم والجزم به ، كدلالة سيارة الإسعاف على المريض في المثال السابق . وكدلالة الأنين على المرض، في القضايا الطبيعية ، ودلالة اللفظ المنبعث من شخص في الظلام على وجود كائن حي، في القضايا العقلية . فإن قوة اللزوم التي بين كل منهما ، تجعل العقل يتصور المرض بمجرد تصور الأنين . ويتصور الكائن الحي بمجرد سماع اللفظ المنبعث في الظلام ، دون حاجة إلى التفكر في الرابطة بينهما وهذا القسم الثالث أقواها من حيث الدلالة وقوة البرهان ، يليه القسم الثاني . أما القسم الأول وهو التلازم غير البيّن . فهو باستقلاله لا يعتبر برهاناً ، حتى يضاف إليه برهان آخر يكشف عن صدق التلازم كما ذكرنا
Third: the evident implication in the narrower sense: that conceiving the implicant alone is sufficient to conceive and be certain of the implication, like the implication of an ambulance indicating a patient in the previous example. And like the implication of groaning indicating illness in natural matters, and the implication of a word emanating from a person in darkness indicating the existence of a living being in intellectual matters. For the strength of the implication between each of them makes the intellect conceive the illness merely by conceiving the groaning, and conceive the living being merely by hearing the word emanating in darkness, without needing to reflect on the connection between them. And this third type is the strongest in terms of indication and strength of proof, followed by the second type. As for the first type, the non-evident implication, it alone is not considered a proof until another proof is added to it revealing the truth of the implication, as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the third and strongest type: luzum bayyin bil-ma'na al-akhass (evident in the narrower sense). Here, merely conceiving the implicant (malzum) is enough to grasp the implication with certainty. Examples: an ambulance implies a patient; groaning implies illness (natural matters); a voice in darkness implies a living being (intellectual matters). The strength of the connection makes the intellect immediately jump to the implied meaning without reflection. The passage then summarizes the strength order: third type strongest, second type next, and the first type alone is not a proof until supplemented.

Key Terms
لزوم بيّن بالمعنى الأخص
سيارة إسعاف
أنين
ظلام
قوة اللزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All implications are equally strong.
Correction: The passage explicitly ranks them: the third type is strongest, the second follows, and the first alone is not a proof.

Checkpoint
Which type of implication is considered the strongest according to the passage?
A) Non-evident implication
B) Evident in the broader sense
C) Evident in the narrower sense
D) All are equal

Key Takeaway
Evident implication in the narrower sense is the strongest logical connection: the sign alone guarantees the conclusion without further reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you trusted a sign so strongly that you acted without a second thought. According to this lesson, was that trust warranted? What does it reveal about your reliance on evident implications?
?
The author says non-evident implication is not a proof until supplemented. In your own search for truth, are you building your beliefs on signs that you have not verified? Which beliefs might be based on weak implications?
?
If the strongest proof is one where the sign itself suffices for certainty, what does that say about the signs you rely on in your daily life? Will you examine them more carefully after this lesson?

The Second Method: Al-Qiyas in Islamic Theology
Defines Islamic qiyas and distinguishes it from Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what Islamic qiyas is and why it is distinct from the Greek logical syllogism.

Core Idea
Islamic qiyas is Quran-inspired, not Greek logic.

Dr. al-Buti introduces the second method of theological proof: analogy (al-qiyas). He immediately clarifies a common confusion: this is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy. Rather, it is the analogical reasoning developed by Islamic jurists and theologians, inspired directly by the Qur'an. Let's examine this distinction carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the author's strong negation: 'ليس المقصود به القياس المنطقي'. This signals a correction.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two types of qiyas
The passage contrasts two things: the Greek logical syllogism and the Islamic analogy. Identify what each is based on.
Greek: propositions and figures. Islamic: inspiration from the Qur'an.
3
Understand the source of Islamic qiyas
Note that Islamic qiyas comes from the scholars of usul al-fiqh and usul al-din (theologians), and its inspiration is the Book of Allah. This roots it in revelation, not human reason alone.
4
Ask why this distinction matters
Consider why the author would need to clarify this. Many might think Islamic reasoning borrowed from Greek philosophy. This passage guards against that misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني القياس : وليس المقصود به القياس المنطقي المقتبس من الفلسفة اليونانية ، والقائم على القضايا والأشكال ، إنما المقصود به ذلك القياس الذي اصطلح عليه علماء أصول الفقه الإسلامي وعلماء أصول الدين ( المتكلمون ) بعد أن استلهموه من كتاب الله عز وجل
The second method: Analogy (al-qiyas). What is intended by it is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy, based on propositions and figures. Rather, what is intended is that analogy which was conventionally adopted by scholars of Islamic jurisprudence and scholars of the principles of religion (the theologians) after they derived inspiration from the Book of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method of proof in kalam: al-qiyas. It clarifies that the intended qiyas is not the logical syllogism of Greek philosophy (based on propositions and figures), but rather the analogical reasoning developed by Islamic jurists (usul al-fiqh) and theologians (mutakallimun), which was inspired by the Quran. This distinction emphasizes that Islamic qiyas has its own roots and is not simply imported from Greek logic.

Key Terms
قياس
منطق
فلسفة يونانية
أصول الفقه
أصول الدين
متكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Islamic qiyas is the same as the Greek logical syllogism.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Islamic qiyas is not the Greek syllogism but rather an analogical reasoning inspired by the Qur'an and developed by Islamic scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source of Islamic qiyas?
A) Greek philosophy
B) The Book of Allah (Qur'an)
C) Aristotelian logic
D) The consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
Islamic qiyas is a distinct method of reasoning rooted in the Qur'an, not a borrowed Greek syllogism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reason about your faith, do you unconsciously rely on methods shaped by non-Islamic sources? How can you ensure your reasoning is truly rooted in the Quran?
?
The scholars derived qiyas from the Quran itself. In what area of your life are you using outside frameworks instead of letting the Quran shape your thinking?
?
This lesson reveals that Islamic methodology has its own authentic origin. Will you now commit to learning the Quranic ways of reasoning, rather than borrowing from the world?

Qiyas in Islamic Theology: Definition, Principles, and Method
Buti defines qiyas, distinguishes it from Greek logic, states its two principles, and explains induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic concept of qiyas as distinct from Greek logic, its two foundational principles (causality and cosmic harmony), and the essential role of induction in establishing certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Islamic qiyas is not Greek logic; it is a method rooted in causality and induction from the Qur'an.

Buti begins by clarifying that the qiyas used in Islamic theology is not the Aristotelian syllogism familiar from Greek philosophy. Instead, it is a method derived from the Qur'an and employed by Islamic jurists and theologians. This passage defines qiyas, explains its two axiomatic principles, and describes how induction is necessary to apply it correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Read the opening lines where Buti explicitly distinguishes Islamic qiyas from Greek logical syllogism. Note that Islamic qiyas is based on extracting causes, not on formal propositional forms.
This distinction is crucial to avoid confusion between the two methods.
2
Identify the two principles
Find the law of causality (every effect has a cause) and the law of harmony and order (the universe's diverse phenomena are connected by universal causes). Both are presented as self-evident axioms.
Buti emphasizes that these principles need no proof; they are intellectual axioms.
3
Grasp the role of induction
See how induction (istiqra') is the tool that reveals the cause and establishes constant relations. The passage describes the process of tracing particulars and observing constant accompaniment and reversal.
Complete induction is required, meaning you must examine all relevant particulars.
4
Recognize the condition for certainty
Buti warns that mere constant concomitance (cause and effect always together) is not enough; you must also verify that the cause is truly effective through certain proof, as coincidence or another factor could be involved.
This condition is especially important when building creed and certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسلك الثاني القياس : وليس المقصود به القياس المنطقي المقتبس من الفلسفة اليونانية ، والقائم على القضايا والأشكال ، إنما المقصود به ذلك القياس الذي اصطلح عليه علماء أصول الفقه الإسلامي وعلماء أصول الدين ( المتكلمون ) بعد أن استلهموه من كتاب الله عز وجل . وهو منهج يتلخص في استخراج علة الشيء أو سببه ، ثم تلمسه فيما قد يشبهه من الأشياء المجهولة ، حتى إذا استيقن الباحث اشتراك كل من المعلوم والمجهول في علة واحدة ، قاس الثاني على الأول في حكمه المنبثق من تأثير تلك العلة . وتقوم فكرة القياس على مبدأين اثنين ، كل منهما من المسلمات العقلية التي لا تحتاج إلى برهان عليها : المبدأ الأول : قانون العلية ، أي إن لكل معلول علة ولكل أثر مؤثراً . المبدأ الثاني : قانون التناسق والنظام في العالم ، أي إن المظاهر الجزئية للكون وإن اختلفت أشكالها ، ترتبط بعلل كلية من شأنها أن تثبت التناسق والانسجام فيما بينها ، ومهما أوغلت في التدقيق بطبائع هذه العلل رأيتها تتجمع أخيراً في أقل عدد من العلل والأسباب . وإنما ينقدح القياس من هذين المبدأين أيضاً ، بواسطة الاستقراء ، إذ هو الذي يبصّر الباحث بحقيقة العلة ، ثم هو الذي يمكن بواسطته إدراك العلاقات الثابتة الكلية بين الأشياء المتناثرة أو المختلفة في الظاهر . وهكذا نلاحظ أن الاستقراء التام شرط أساسي لابد منه لكل من برهاني التلازم والقياس . وكيفية الاستقراء هنا هي أن تتتبع جزئيات ما ادعيت أنه علة لأمر معين ، فتجده لا ينفك عن إنتاج معلوله ، وذلك بأن تتأمل العلاقة بين العلة والمعلول فترى فيها ظاهرة الاطراد والانعكاس . أي كلما وجدت العلة وجد المعلول ، وكلما فقدت العلة فقد المعلول . ثم تمعن النظر بعد ذلك في العلة فتراها مؤثرة في المعلول بالبرهان اليقيني ، إذ قد يكون هذا الاطراد أو الانعكاس بينهما لمحض المصادفة أو لعامل آخر . وبذلك تعلم أن شرط القياس هنا ( أي في بناء العقيدة والقضايا اليقينية )
The second method: qiyas (analogical reasoning). What is intended by it is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy, which is based on propositions and figures; rather, what is intended is that qiyas which the scholars of Usul al-Fiqh and the scholars of Usul al-Din (the mutakallimun) agreed upon after deriving it from the Book of God Almighty. It is a method that consists in extracting the cause or reason of a thing, then seeking it in what may resemble it among unknown things, until when the researcher is certain of the sharing of both the known and the unknown in one cause, he analogises the second to the first in its ruling emanating from the influence of that cause. The idea of qiyas is based on two principles, each of which is among the intellectual axioms that need no proof: the first principle: the law of causality (qanun al-‘illiyya), i.e., every effect has a cause and every trace has an influence. The second principle: the law of harmony and order in the world (qanun al-tanasuq wa al-nizam fi al-‘alam), i.e., the partial manifestations of the universe, although their forms differ, are connected by universal causes that establish harmony and coherence among them, and no matter how deep you go into scrutiny of the natures of these causes, you see them finally gathering into the smallest number of causes and reasons. Qiyas arises from these two principles also by means of induction (istiqra’), for it is what clarifies to the researcher the reality of the cause, and then it is that by means of which it is possible to perceive the constant universal relations among scattered or apparently different things. Thus we note that complete induction (al-istiqra’ al-tamm) is an essential condition necessary for both the proof of implication (talawum) and qiyas. And the manner of induction here is that you trace the particulars of what you claim is a cause for a certain matter, and you find that it does not cease to produce its effect, and that by reflecting on the relation between cause and effect you see in it the phenomenon of constant accompaniment and reversal: i.e., whenever the cause is found, the effect is found, and whenever the cause is absent, the effect is absent. Then you deeply reflect on the cause and see it as effective in the effect by certain proof, because this constant accompaniment or reversal could be due to pure coincidence or another factor. And thus you know that the condition for qiyas here (i.e., in building creed and certain propositions)
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage defines the second method: qiyas (analogical reasoning). The author first distinguishes it from the Aristotelian logical syllogism; Islamic qiyas is derived from the Qur’an and used by jurists and theologians. The method consists of extracting the cause (‘illa) of a known thing, then looking for that same cause in an unknown thing, and once certainty is reached that both share the cause, the ruling of the known is applied to the unknown. But this procedure rests on two self-evident principles: (1) the law of causality: every effect has a cause; (2) the law of order: the universe is harmoniously connected by universal causes that reduce to a few. Qiyas also requires induction (istiqra’), which reveals the cause and establishes constant relations. Complete induction is essential: you must examine particulars and observe constant concomitance (whenever the cause exists, the effect exists) and reversal (when the cause is absent, the effect is absent). However, this pattern alone is insufficient; you must also verify that the cause is truly effective through certain proof, because concomitance might be coincidental. The passage ends by noting that this condition is especially important for building creed and certain knowledge.

Key Terms
القياس
العلية
الاستقراء
العلة
المعلول
التناسق
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think qiyas in kalam is the same as Aristotelian syllogism.
Correction: Buti explicitly states that Islamic qiyas is not the logical syllogism borrowed from Greek philosophy. It is a method based on causality and induction, derived from the Qur'an.

Checkpoint
What are the two principles on which qiyas is based according to Buti?
A) The law of contradiction and the law of excluded middle
B) The law of causality and the law of harmony and order in the world
C) The principle of identity and the principle of sufficient reason
D) The law of non-contradiction and the law of causality

Key Takeaway
Qiyas in Islamic theology is a method of analogical reasoning based on the law of causality and cosmic order, requiring complete induction and certain proof of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to have certain knowledge about your Creator or your faith, have you personally verified the causes behind that belief through a rigorous method like qiyas, or are you relying on patterns that might be coincidental? Will you commit to tracing every core belief back to its cause?
?
The law of harmony and order says the universe is coherent and its causes reduce to a few. Is your own spiritual life coherent, or is it fragmented by contradictions? What one contradiction in your practice will you resolve today to align with this principle?
?
Buti states that complete induction—examining all particulars—is necessary for certain knowledge in creed. Are there areas of your deen where you have accepted a teaching without fully investigating its evidence? Name one and decide now: will you fill that gap, or will you remain willfully ignorant?

Three Conditions for Certain Qiyas
Defines the three conditions for qiyas to yield certainty in creedal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three essential conditions for analogical reasoning (qiyas) to produce certainty in creedal matters, and why probabilistic analogy is insufficient for theology.

Core Idea
Certain analogy requires an effective, constant, and clear cause.

In this passage, Ramadan al-Buti outlines the rigorous standards required for analogical reasoning when dealing with matters of creed. He explains that for an analogy to yield certainty, the cause ('illa) must meet three strict criteria. If any condition is lacking, the analogy is only probabilistic and cannot be used in theology, though it may be acceptable in practical legal rulings. The passage also provides a vivid example to illustrate how this reasoning works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation once to get the overall argument. Notice the three conditions and the example.
2
Identify the three conditions
Look for the three conditions listed: effectiveness (ta'thir), constancy and reversibility (itrad wa in'ikas), and clarity and precision (indibat). Understand each one.
The footnote clarifies what is meant by 'effectiveness'.
3
Distinguish between certain and probabilistic analogy
Notice how the author distinguishes between qiyas yaqini (certain) and qiyas dhanni (probabilistic). Understand why only the former is acceptable in creedal matters.
4
Explore the example
Work through the example of seeing dwellings and inferring water. See how the conditions are met: water is effective, constant, and clear.
This helps solidify the abstract conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو أن تكون العلة مؤثرة في المعلول ) ، وأن تكون مطردة ومنعكسة وأن تكون منضبطة واضحة غير مضطربة .
فإذا تدانت العلة عن مستوى هذا الشرط ، كأن لم يتضح فيها التأثير ، وإنما تجلت فيها ملاءمة ما مع المعلول ، فهو قياس ظني ، لا يقبل في الأحكام الاعتقادية والعقلية ، وإنما يمكن أن يقبل في المسائل الفقهية العملية ، لقيام الدليل القاطع على أن الأدلة الظنية فيها كافية للتعبد والأحكام الشرعية كما أسلفنا ، فيكفي في القياس للأحكام الشرعية العملية أن تكون العلة منضبطة ومطردة منعكسة ، ولكن لا يشترط فيها أن تكون مؤثرة بل يكفي أن تكون ملائمة في اجتهاد الباحث لبناء حكم عليها . فالقياس في الشرعيات العملية يختلف في الحقيقة اختلافاً كبيراً عن طبيعة القياس وشروطه في المسائل الاعتقادية . مثال ذلك أن تبصر على البعد دوراً أو خياماً يسكنها الناس ، فتستيقن من ذلك وجود الماء في ذلك المكان فإن سبيل اليقين بذلك ، أنك تستحضر في ذهنك بسرعة خاطفة سائر ما تعلمه من الأماكن التي يعيش فيها الناس ، فترى أن من أهم أسباب صلاحيتها لمقامهم ، توافر الماء فيها ؛ لا يختلف هذا المعنى بحال من الأحوال ، كما تلاحظ تأثير السبب الذي هو الماء في المسبب الذي هو العيش وإمكان الحياة
وعندئذ تقيس هذا المكان الذي يلوح لك من بعيد على تلك الأماكن الأخرى وتجزم بوجود الماء فيه وإن لم تره عيناك
.
(۱) نقصد بالتأثير أن يثبت بالبرهان سببية العلة للمعلول ، كسببية المطر للإنبات والنار للإحراق ، بقطع النظر عن البحث في حقيقة هذه السببية وتحليلها على ضوء الإيمان بالمسب الحقيقي جل جلاله ومكان البحث في تحليل ذلك ، الحديث عن قانون السببية في الكون فارجع إليه في مكانه من هذا الكتاب
It (the cause) must be effective in the effect, and must be constant and reversible, and must be well-defined, clear, and not ambiguous. If the cause falls short of this condition, such that its effectiveness is not clear, and only some suitability with the effect appears, then it is a probabilistic analogy (qiyas dhanni), which is not accepted in creedal and rational judgments. Rather, it can be accepted in practical jurisprudential matters, because decisive evidence has established that probabilistic evidences are sufficient for worship and legal rulings, as we have mentioned earlier. So for analogy in practical legal rulings, it is sufficient that the cause be well-defined, constant, and reversible, but it is not required that it be effective; rather, it is sufficient that it be suitable in the researcher's ijtihad for building a ruling upon it. Thus analogy in practical legal matters actually differs greatly from the nature of analogy and its conditions in creedal matters. An example of that: you see at a distance a house or tents inhabited by people, and you become certain of the presence of water in that place. The way to certainty in that is that you bring to mind in a flash all the places you know where people live, and you see that one of the most important reasons for their suitability for habitation is the availability of water; this meaning does not differ in any way. Moreover, you observe the effect of the cause, which is water, in the effect, which is living and the possibility of life. Then you analogize this place that appears to you from afar to those other places, and you affirm the presence of water in it even though you have not seen it with your own eyes. (1) By 'effect' we mean that it is proven by demonstration that the cause is causal for the effect, like the causality of rain for growth and fire for burning, regardless of the inquiry into the reality of this causality and its analysis in light of belief in the True Cause (Exalted be His Majesty). The place for research on that analysis is the discussion on the law of causality in the universe; refer to it in its place in this book.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions that must be met for analogical reasoning (qiyas) to yield certainty in creedal and rational matters. The author, Ramadan al-Buti, states three essential conditions for the cause ('illa): it must be effective (mu'aththira) in the effect, meaning proven causality; it must be constant and reversible (mutarida wa mun'akisa), applying in all cases and not contradicted; and it must be well-defined and clear (mundabita waadiha), not ambiguous. If the cause does not fully meet these conditions, the analogy is probabilistic (dhanni) and cannot be used in creedal or intellectual matters, only in practical legal rulings where probabilistic evidence is sufficient. The author then gives an example: seeing dwellings in the distance leads to certain knowledge of water because the cause (water) is effective for human habitation. The reasoning involves recalling known places where water is essential. The footnote clarifies that 'effectiveness' here refers to proven causality by demonstration, setting aside the deeper theological debate about the ultimate Cause (Allah).

Key Terms
علة
تأثير
مطردة ومنعكسة
منضبطة
قياس ظني
قياس يقيني
الأحكام الاعتقادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All analogies are equally valid and yield certainty.
Correction: Only analogies with a cause that is effective, constant, and clear produce certainty; those with merely suitable causes are probabilistic and not valid in creedal matters.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the main difference between qiyas in creedal matters and qiyas in practical legal matters?
A) In legal matters, the cause must be effective, while in creed it needs only suitability.
B) In creedal matters, the cause must be effective, constant, and well-defined to yield certainty, while in legal matters, suitability and constancy are sufficient.
C) Both require the same conditions.
D) In legal matters, qiyas is not allowed.

Key Takeaway
For analogy to yield certainty in creedal matters, the cause must be effective, constant, and clear; otherwise, it is only probabilistic and unsuitable for theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Islam that you have not thoroughly examined. On what kind of evidence does it rest—certain or probabilistic? Are you willing to test it against the three conditions of effective, constant, and clear cause? What would change if you did?
?
You routinely make judgments about people and situations using analogies. When was the last time you assumed something about someone based on a similarity to a past experience? Did you treat that assumption as certain or probable? How might the distinction between certain and probabilistic analogy change the way you judge others?
?
The author says probabilistic analogy is acceptable in matters of worship but not in matters of creed. Consider: is there any aspect of your personal faith—something you believe about Allah, the Prophet, or the afterlife—that you have accepted based on a mere suitability or feeling rather than a clear, constant, effective proof? What would it cost you to scrutinize it now?

Certain vs Probabilistic Qiyas
Contrasts probabilistic and certain qiyas with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between probabilistic and certain qiyas through the water and design examples.

Core Idea
Certain qiyas requires necessary causality, not mere suitability.

This lesson clarifies a critical distinction in Islamic reasoning: when does analogy yield certainty? The author contrasts two scenarios—inferring people from water (mere conjecture) and inferring a Maker from design (certain). This distinction is foundational for evaluating theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the reversed water example
Read the passage where seeing water leads to inferring people. Note why this is only probabilistic: the causality is not effective but merely suitable.
This contrasts with the earlier certain example of inferring water from people.
2
Study the design argument
Examine the statement that the mark of craftsmanship indicates a Maker, because the effect cannot separate from its cause. This is certain qiyas.
This is a classic kalam argument for God's existence.
3
Compare the two types of qiyas
Identify the key difference: necessary causation (design) vs mere appropriateness (water-people). Certain qiyas requires a real causal link.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما لو عكست النظر في هذا المثال ذاته. كأن أبصرت على البعد بريق ماء ، ففهمت من درجة اليقين ذلك وجود أناس يعيشون هناك ؛ فإنه مجرد ظن لا يمكن أن يرقى إلى ذلك
وذلك لأن علية الماء لحياة الإنسان حقيقة ثابتة بدلالة التأثير فلابد من وجود الماء حيث يوجد الناس . أما علية الماء لوجود الناس من حوله فمحض
مناسبة وملاءمة
ومثاله أيضاً ، دلالة كل مافيه مظهر الصنعة والتدبير ، على وجود صانع ومدبّر له . ضرورة أن المعلول لا ينفك عن علته
ومن هنا تعلم أن علماء المسلمين إنما يتبعون المنهج الاستقرائي في كل مالم يمكن إخضاعه للتجربة والمشاهدة ، وفي ظل هذا المنهج يلتقي كل من الالتزام والقياس . وهو كما ترى أبعد ما يكون عن الاستنتاجات الغيبية والتأملات المجردة التي أوغلت فيها الفلسفة اليونانية أيما إيغال .
بل قد علم كل من تأمل في المنهج الإسلامي للبحث ، أن علماء الإسلام لا يمكن أن يقيموا أي حكم عقلي أو عقيدي إلا على أساس الحقيقة التي تجمعت فيها كل مقومات اليقين
•
فأما تلك الحقائق الأخرى التي ظلت مستورة وراء حجاب الشكوك ولم تطلها إلا يد الاستنتاج النظري ، كتلك التي تلوح خلال دراسات تاريخية أو آثار مكتشفة أو مستحاثات قديمة - فما عرف التاريخ الإسلامي أن حقيقة يقينية ما ، قد أقيمت فوقها أو أنها اتخذت برهان نقد أو استدلال أو بناء فكر . ولكنها ظلت عندهم بحثاً غير موصول وشكاً يطوف حولـه كل احتمال ، وسبيلاً يدعو لمواصلة السير إلى نهايته بخطاً من البحث الاستقرائي السديد
تلك
هي
✩
✩
خلاصة سريعة جداً ، عن المنهج العلمي للبحث عند المسلمين
As for if you reverse the consideration in this same example, such that you see at a distance the glimmer of water, and you understand from that with certainty the presence of people living there; then it is mere conjecture (dhann) that cannot rise to certainty. That is because the causality of water for human life is a proven truth by indication of effectiveness, so water must exist wherever people are. As for the causality of water for the presence of people around it, it is mere appropriateness and suitability. Another example of it is the indication of everything that contains the mark of craftsmanship and design, to the existence of a Maker and Designer for it, necessarily because the effect cannot be separated from its cause. From here you learn that Muslim scholars follow the inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqra'i) in everything that cannot be subjected to experiment and observation. Under this method, both certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas) meet. And it is, as you see, far removed from the occult speculations and abstract contemplations that Greek philosophy indulged in excessively. Rather, everyone who has reflected on the Islamic method of research knows that Muslim scholars cannot establish any rational or creedal judgment except on the basis of truth that has gathered all elements of certainty. As for those other truths that have remained veiled behind the screen of doubts and have not been reached except by the hand of theoretical deduction, such as those that appear through historical studies, discovered artifacts, or ancient fossils, Islamic history has never known that a certain truth was established upon them or that they were taken as proof for criticism, inference, or intellectual construction. Rather, they remained for them an unresolved research and a doubt around which every possibility hovers, and a path that calls for continuing the journey to its end with steps of sound inductive research. That is the very brief summary of the scientific method of research according to Muslims.
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of qiyas by contrasting certain and probabilistic analogy. The author presents a reversed scenario: seeing water and inferring people is only probabilistic, because the causality is not effective—people may not be present near every body of water. He then gives the classic argument for God's existence: the design in the universe indicates a Maker, because the effect (design) necessarily implies a cause (designer). This is certain qiyas. The author then explains the Islamic inductive method (manhaj istiqra'i), which combines certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas). He emphasizes that this method is far from Greek philosophical speculation. Muslim scholars only base judgments on truths that have all elements of certainty (yakin). They reject speculative historical evidence, such as artifacts or fossils, as insufficient for certainty, considering them as unresolved research that requires further inductive investigation. The passage concludes by calling this a brief summary of the Islamic scientific method.

Key Terms
قياس ظني
قياس يقيني
مظهر الصنعة
ضَرُورَة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is inferring people from the glimmer of water only conjecture?
A) Because water is not necessary for life.
B) Because the causality of water for the presence of people is mere suitability, not effective causation.
C) Because people never live near water.
D) Because the example is reversed.

Key Takeaway
Certain qiyas requires a necessary causal link, whereas probabilistic qiyas relies on mere suitability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you rely on mere suitability (like the water example) or on necessary causation (like the design example) to recognize the Creator? What would change if you treated the evidence as merely probable?
?
Your daily life is filled with inferences: from patterns to causes. Which of your beliefs about God rest on necessary reasoning, and which on mere appropriateness? Are you willing to examine each?
?
If the existence of design necessarily implies a Designer, why do you sometimes live as if the world runs on its own? What would it look like to truly live as if every crafted thing cries out for its Maker?

The Islamic Inductive Method
Explains the Islamic inductive method and its epistemic standards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic inductive method and why Muslim scholars reject speculative history as a basis for certainty.

Core Idea
Islamic method builds on certain evidence, not abstract speculation.

After distinguishing certain and probabilistic qiyas, the author now explains the broader Islamic inductive method (al-manhaj al-istiqra'i). This method combines certainty and analogy while rejecting Greek speculative philosophy. He also outlines what kinds of evidence are acceptable for establishing creedal truths.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Islamic inductive method
Read how the method applies to things beyond experiment: it combines certain knowledge (iltizam) and analogy (qiyas) and avoids occult speculation.
The author contrasts this with Greek philosophy.
2
Examine the requirement of certainty
Note that Muslim scholars only base rulings on truths that 'gather all elements of certainty.' This excludes theoretical deduction.
3
Analyze the rejection of speculative history
Read the list of uncertain evidence: historical studies, artifacts, fossils. These are considered unresolved research, not proof.
This shows the strict epistemological standard.

Misconception & Correction

D) They are used as primary evidence for creedal judgments.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic inductive method only accepts truths with all elements of certainty, treating speculative evidence as unresolved research.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says Muslim scholars never based a certain truth on speculative historical evidence. Think of a belief you hold about Islamic history that you consider absolutely certain. What is the foundation of that certainty? Is it based on transmitted texts with all elements of certainty, or on academic theories and interpretations? If the latter, will you reclassify that belief as 'unresolved research' rather than a settled creed?
?
This passage sets a high bar: only truths with all elements of certainty can justify a rational or creedal judgment. When you argue about religion with others, do you often rely on evidence that is actually speculative (e.g., a historical claim or a philosophical argument)? Are you willing to only present arguments that meet this standard, even if it means being silent on many issues? What would that cost you?
?
The source calls speculative evidence 'a path that calls for continuing the journey to its end with steps of sound inductive research.' Is there an area of your knowledge—perhaps about the early Muslim community, or the nature of existence—where you have settled for probability instead of pursuing certainty? What concrete step will you take this week to move that topic from doubt towards sound inductive investigation?
